Hi, I am Kalyan Reddy Dida and I will be your instructor in this course.

So what is Docker daemon client image container and then Docker hub.
So this is a theory part in this entire course.

Docker container using that Docker Hub, and after that we will also go ahead and then build a Docker

a practical demo.

need to use docker file instruction add, and at what situations we need to use copy.

You can fetch the data from a URL and copy it in your Docker image.

in GitHub, and that one you are going to fetch using this URL and then demonstrate.

=== Demo-01:  Install Docker Desktop, Sign Up to Docker Hub ===


--- Why Docker? ---

Welcome back.
Let's understand why we need to use Docker in this lecture.
Right.
So let's understand what problems we have with the traditional infra right.
So in traditional infra we will first provision the hardware infrastructure means like we will go and
then purchase the servers and then place them in our data center.
Right.
And then we install the operating system on those servers.
And after that we will install the libraries and dependencies required for our application on that operating
system.
And after that we will deploy our databases, app servers and web servers.
So we will install them manually on that respective server.
So these are the things we are going to do in our traditional infra.
Right.
So this traditional approach for every server if you want to do these things it is going to be time
consuming and need to perform Install config on every server and every environment like dev, QA, staging
and then production.
And in addition to that, when we talk about compatibility and dependency perspective, we need to keep
resolving issues related to libraries and dependencies on different servers.
Right.
So and also we might have inconsistencies across environment.
And it is very hard to track across dev, QA, staging and prod environments.
And they end up with some inconsistencies.
Right.
So it will be very difficult to manage all these things.
And there is a lot of manpower needed to manage all these physical servers.
And also when we talk about operational support.
So we need more resources to handle operational issues on day to day basis server support with both
hardware and software, which means for the operating system.
Also we need to do patching and all those things.
So it takes time and we need to schedule them.
We need to schedule the downtime for our applications to upgrade the servers.
All those things are going to take time and example if we take the development environments, if a new
developer joins the team, the time it takes to provision his development environment in traditional
approach is also going to take a lot of time.
So let's say after these physical infrastructure, we already got something called virtual infrastructure.
So let's see first what our physical machines.
So you have a hardware infrastructure.
You have operating system.
And you install libraries and dependencies required by your applications.
And you install applications in physical machines.
So after physical machines it it got evolved to virtual machines.
And in virtual machines what happened is on top of hardware infrastructure, a hypervisor is present.
And on top of that we create a virtual machine, and in virtual machine we are going to have the operating
system.
And in operating system we are going to deploy the libraries and then dependencies related to our applications.
And we can clone one virtual machine and recreate multiple virtual machines.
All those things are available, right?
So once we have physical machines are very tough to manage.
And it got simplified with virtual machines.
Now let us see physical machines with Docker how it is going to be there, and also virtual machines
with Docker how it is going to be there.
So on hardware infrastructure, in operating system, we will install the operating system on the server,
and after that we will install the Docker engine.
So on that physical server we will install the Docker engine.
And using that Docker engine.
Right.
This Docker engine will run the Containers so we can create a nginx container, app, service container
or database server containers.
We can create them directly and to to replicate these containers.
It is going to be very quick.
It is like Subseconds.
You can create 100 web server containers within some seconds.
We can create 100 app server containers.
So that's the flexibility of Docker.
So if there is no Docker engine here, and these three applications are installed directly on this operating
system in this physical machine, to replicate this physical machine to 100 machines, it is going to
cost hugely for us.
And in addition to that, there is a lot of manpower needed.
But if you see here, if your hardware infrastructure supports hundreds of things here using Docker,
then it becomes super simple.
And if we see about virtual machines in virtual machines, you will have the hardware infrastructure
and a hypervisor.
And on top of that you will create virtual machines.
And in each virtual machine you are going to have operating system.
And then you will install Docker Engine in those virtual machines.
And then you can deploy your applications as containers in those virtual machines.
So this is about where Docker comes like in physical infrastructure also or in virtual machine infrastructure.
Also you need to install the Docker engine on that respective operating system for the Docker containers
to to to create Docker containers in Docker right now.
Advantages of using Docker.
So let's start why we need to use containers.
So those are flexible.
So any complex application can be containerized.
And it is that advanced nowadays.
And these containers are very very lightweight.
These use very fewer resources than virtual machines.
So virtual machines need lot of packages for the base to work.
But here when we talk about containers, it is like a very minor small operating system.
Present inside.
The slimmer version of operating system is present inside a Docker container wherein only the application
related things, whatever is required to run on that respective operating system inside container will
be present.
Other than that, all on all, all on all not needed packages will be excluded from that.
Okay.
So that is the reason.
It is very lightweight and it is also portable.
So you can build locally, deploy to cloud and run anywhere these containers.
And in addition to that these are very very loosely coupled.
So you can replace or upgrade containers without affecting others.
And in addition to that these are highly scalable if we talk about these containers, you can create
you can create one container and you can create a replica of equivalent container to 100 containers.
Right.
So why?
Because you have the underlying Docker image.
So we'll learn about what is container.
What is Docker image in the next architecture lecture.
But we with single Docker image you can create hundreds of Docker containers which means like a replica
of one container.
You can create hundreds of containers easily with simple commands.
And these are highly secure.
Containers provide isolation without extra setup.
And also this is a minimal OS without any, uh, major amount of packages present inside it.
It will be highly secure.
So that's about Docker on a high level.
In our next lecture we will focus on learning about Docker architecture.
I will see you in the next lecture.
Until then, bye bye.
Thank you.


=== Demo-02: Docker Commands: pullm run, exec, stop, start, rm, rmi ===


--- Docker Architecture or Terminology ---

Welcome back.
In this lecture we are going to know about Docker architecture or Docker terminology.
So let's understand what all things involved in Docker on a high level.
So let's start with Docker host where we install our Docker engine.
Right.
So it might be a virtual machine or a physical machine.
So Docker host can be a virtual machine or a physical machine.
And inside that we will have the Docker daemon.
So this Docker daemon listens for Docker API requests and manages Docker objects such as Docker images,
Docker containers, Docker networks, and Docker volumes.
So that's about Docker daemon.
And we will also have something called Docker client.
And this Docker client can be present on either Docker host or any other machine like your local desktop.
Now this Docker host can be even your local desktop.
Also not only server are not only virtual machine, even our local desktop also can be a Docker host
and Docker client be client can be installed on either Docker host, or.
You can also install the Docker client only on your local machine and using the client only, we will
be connecting to this Docker host and whatever the commands we run using this Docker client.
Those will be uh, executed using Docker daemon.
The Docker client is the primary way that many Docker users interact with Docker, and the Docker client
is nothing but Docker.
So if you go to the terminal and type docker space images, it will list your images.
So the CLI name is Docker.
And when you use commands such as docker run, the client sends these commands to docker d, which is
nothing but your Docker daemon which carries them out, which means docker daemon executes those commands
and the Docker daemon uses the Docker API.
The Docker client can communicate with more than one daemon, so you have installed Docker in multiple
hosts, and you can point Docker client to specific host and then run the command.
So that's the Docker client.
Now let's also talk about Docker images right.
So we have discussed Docker daemon and we have discussed Docker client and also Docker host.
Now we are going to talk about Docker images.
So these Docker image is a read only template with instructions for creating a Docker container.
Right.
So you know when we talk about virtual machines right.
So we will have the virtual machine images.
And we take that virtual machine image and then we create a virtual machine server from it.
In the same way we have a Docker image here.
And using this Docker image we will create a runnable instances which are called Docker containers.
So often an image is based on another image with some additional customization.
So this is one important thing.
So an image is based on another image with some additional customizations.
So for example we want to add custom configurations in our nginx web server.
Then we take the base nginx image and modify as per our need.
Right.
So we will do this practical demo in demo three where we build a Docker image.
Understand this statement here right.
So a image is based on another image.
Or we want to add custom configurations in nginx.
Then we take a base nginx image and modify as per our need what exactly that means.
So let's go to hub.docker.com and verify and search for nginx docker image.
So we take this as the base nginx image and then we modify it as per our needs.
So this is the base nginx image.
And let us go to the demo three and give a quick overview about it.
So let me go to demo three and here we will write a docker file saying from nginx.
So here we are taking the base nginx image.
And on top of that we are customizing based on our needs.
So that is what it means.
So now let's come back here.
And next move on to the next thing here.
Right.
So which is Docker containers a Docker container is a runnable instance of an image.
So once you create your custom Docker image so you can run this image as a container.
So which means the live process or live application will be running inside this container.
So you can access your application by accessing this container endpoint.
So we can create a container or start a container.
Stop a container.
Move or delete a container using Docker API or CLI.
So these are pretty new here the terminology.
But when we go and then implement with multiple practical demos.
This is going to be easy for us, and we can connect a container to one or more networks, attached
storage to the container, and even create new image based on its current state.
All these things we can do from container, right?
And when a container is removed, any changes to its state that are not stored in persistent storage
will disappear, which means by default, container will have only ephemeral storage.
It's a temporary storage.
When the container is stopped, the data is gone until and unless it has a persistent storage associated
with that.
And we are going to also attach persistent storage for this containers in the concept of volumes, bind
mounts and then tmpfs mounts.
And for those things also we have dedicated demos planned.
Here you can see volumes and bind mounts and tmpfs mounts.
So those things we are going to do practically from volumes perspective.
And then now we have learned about Docker.
Docker daemon.
Docker client and Docker images and Docker container.
Finally, we need to learn about Docker Hub here.
Right?
So whatever the Docker images we have right.
Those will be ideally stored in the Docker Hub.
Right.
So this is the registry we call it as the Docker Hub.
You can see huge amount of Docker images present.
And we download the Docker images from this registry and run them as containers.
So for example you created a new Docker image.
You push it and store that respective Docker image in this Docker Hub.
And Docker Hub is a central repository for you.
And when you want to run a container, you pull that Docker image from that registry and run it as a
container.
So once you build your Docker image locally on your local desktop and push it to Docker Hub, it is
in the central repository.
So you can download that from any desktop and run it from any desktop or from any server.
Right.
So for that purpose you store those images in a central repository which is called Docker Hub.
Right.
So using docker pull command or docker run command you can download the Docker images.
Right.
So a Docker registry stores Docker images.
Docker hub is public registry that anyone can use.
And Docker is configured to look for images on Docker Hub by default.
And we can even run our own private registry.
Also, not only the public Docker Hub registry, we can also host our own private registry using Docker.
And when we use the Docker pull or Docker run commands, the required images are pulled from our configured
registry.
When we use the docker push command, our image, our Docker image, which we have built in our local
host, will be configured to push to that respective Docker Hub.
This completes the discussion of learning or understanding about Docker terminology.

=== Demo-04: Dockerfile - LABELS Instruction ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to docker pull, run, exec, stop, start, rm, rmi commands ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to pull the Docker image from Docker Hub and run it.
So let's see what we are going to do in a visual manner.
So we have a Docker Hub or a Docker registry.
And there we have the Docker image my nginx.
And we have v1 v2 and v3 and then v4 version of this nginx image.
And what we are going to do from our local Docker desktop is we are going to pull that Docker image
to our local desktop, and once we pull it, we are going to run that Docker image to create containers.
So the following commands we are going to learn as part of this demo.
So to pull the Docker image we are going to use docker pull command.
And to run the container from the docker image, we are going to use the docker run command.
And to connect to the Docker containers, we are going to use docker exec command.
And to stop or start containers we are going to use docker stop or docker start command.
And in addition to that, to remove a container we are going to use docker rm command.
And finally, to remove the docker image from our local desktop, we are going to run the command docker
rmi.
So let's go ahead and then practically implement it.

So in this demo build a Docker image and push to Docker Hub.
We will have a folder called Docker Files.
And inside that you will create a docker file with name as capital d o c k e r f I l e.

So this Docker file automates the process of creating Docker images by specifying steps which are called
instructions.

So why do you use this Docker files.
So like in your Docker image if you want to install the software, copy some files or set environmental
variables or run some commands and also run your application.

=== Demo-12: Dockerfile - HEALTHCHECK Instruction ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to Dockerfile LABELS ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about Docker file label instruction.
So what is a label instruction in Docker file.
So this label instruction adds the metadata to our Docker image.
So an image can have more than one label.
So labels included in the base image are inherited by our image.
So earlier in the previous demo also we have created a custom Docker image.
So wherein we have used the base Docker image as nginx where we have specified from nginx and built
our custom Docker image.
So whatever is present in the base image will be also inherited in our custom Docker image.
And if a label already exists but with a different value, the most recently applied value when building
the Docker image.
So those will be Override it with the newly set value.
So in zero four docker file label section in Docker files folder, you will find the docker file and
index dot HTML.
And inside this you can see the labels information.
So from nginx.
And we are trying to use the simple slim nginx docker image.
So we said nginx colon alpine slim.
Alpine slim is the tag which will be a very small Docker image.
So the base image we are using is a very small one.
And these are the labels we are going to add.
So all the types whatever is available I tried to add it here okay.
So because this is a labels demo I tried to add maximum whatever is available.
So you can define your custom label using this information label and maintainer name and label version,
all these things.
So let us go back here and verify the same thing.
So we'll go here to labels zero for Docker files and Docker file.
And you can see it here.
So this is the nginx alpine slim as the base Docker image.
And in the custom labels we can define maintainer version and then description.
And these are the standard labels which ideally we need to define which is open container initiative
labels.
We call it as OCI labels.
So even this maintainer is deprecated.
But still we many are using it.
But the important thing is it is recommended to use OCI labels open container initiative label.
So here you will define the label as arg dot open containers, dot image dot author.
So you can provide your author name.
You can provide the title, you can provide the description version and any revision number.
If you want, we can provide 1.02.0 and on which date we are creating it.
And what is the image URL, you can provide it and image source where the docker file is present, exactly
where the docker file is present.
You can provide and any documentation you can provide.
And who is the vendor.
You can provide any license information you can provide.
So all these things you can provide inside this OCI label.

=== Demo-14: Docker Ports:  -p and -P flags ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to Dockerfile ADD vs COPY ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about Docker file add and copy instructions and also add versus copy
how it is going to look like.
So let's go ahead and understand what is a copy instruction.
In our previous demos we already used the copy instruction.
But here we are going to learn it on a very detailed way.
So what is copy instruction in docker file.
Copy instruction copies new files or directories from source and adds them to the file system of the
image at the path, which is called destination right now.
One important thing to know about copy instruction is files, and directories can be copied from the
build context, build stage, named context, and also from an image.
So all these things are possible if we are using the copy instruction.
So then what is then add instruction right.
So that also we need to know.
So for Add instruction also the definition is going to be same.
The add instruction copies new files or directories from source and adds them to the file system of
the image at the path destination.
So it's the same thing for both.
But important things are files and directories can be copied from the build context.
So from build context, which is same for both copy and add instructions.
But in add instruction you have some important things, which is you can add the files from a remote
URL.
And also you can add files from a git repository.
So that's the key difference for copy and add.
And in 99.99% of the cases we use copy only.
In rare cases only we use add.
So that's another important thing to remember.
So if you see here this is zero five Docker file add versus copy.
And here you see in the copy instruction in the Docker files folder you have the copy file dot HTML,
which will copy to usr share nginx HTML folder.
So the name is copy hyphen file dot HTML.
So that is copy thing whatever is doing and in the add thing what it is doing is in app files folder
there are multiple files.
And those files we are going to zip it and store it in static files dot tar dot gz in docker files folder.
And now we are going to say add static files.tar.bz two usr share nginx.
HTML.
So what happens is add can also untar the files.
So that's the key advantage of add instruction.
So you have provided a tar file here tar and zip file here.
And when it is creating a Docker image it can enter the files present inside this zip file to this location.
So now if you go back to our Visual Studio Code, here in the app files you have file 152, file 35455
and index dot HTML.
So all these files we will zip it and put it in Docker files folder with the name static files dot tar,
dot xz and that when running this docker build command with this docker file, this add instruction
will enter those files at this location.
So that's additional advantage of using add instruction.
So you can unzip the files during the build time.
So that's the key thing.
Now let's come back and compare both copy versus add.
So from a file transfer perspective copy copies files from build context.
And for add.
Also it is the same thing.
And from tar archive extraction perspective copy command doesn't have that flexibility.
Tar archive extraction is not present in copy command but in add it automatically extracts the dot tar
files.
So that's the advantage of using add instruction in the same way URL support is not present for copy
instruction, but for add instruction we have the URL support from the web and performance.
If you compare.
Copy with ad copy is going to be faster and less overhead, and AD is going to be slower if.
Using additional features like tar and tar and then URL get it from the remote URL.
All those things it is going to be slower and from security.
Also copy is safe for local file transfers and when we are using the remote URLs AD will be a security
risk for us.
So that is another important thing.
And from use case perspective, copy is preferred for all local file copies and ad use only for tar
extraction or URL fetching until and unless it is absolutely needed.
If not, try to use copy in maximum cases.
So that's about copy versus ad instructions in a Docker file.
So let's go ahead and then practically implement it I'll see you in the next lecture.
Until then bye bye.
Thank you.

=== Demo-18:  Docker Volumes: Mount a Volume sub-directroy ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to Dockerfile ARG Instruction ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about arg instruction in docker file.
So what is arg instruction.
So it defines a variable that users can pass at build time to the builder.
So when we are using the docker build command so we can use the hyphen hyphen build arg as the flag
and provide the variable name and the value so that this, whatever the value you have defined in the
Docker file, can be overrided using the build time when we are building the Docker image.
So we can define one or more arguments in our docker file.
So you can see it.
Here we have defined the argument nginx version equal to 1.16.
And here for the from instruction we have said nginx colon and then dollar with flower bracket opening
nginx version hyphen alpine hyphen slim.
So what happens is when we run the docker build command generally without this build argument.
So whatever is present here in this argument will be replaced here.
So it is going to use nginx 1.26 as the base image version when building the Docker image.
And if you want to override that at build time, then you can use.
When you are running the docker build command, you can provide hyphen, hyphen, build arg and provide
the variable name which is nginx underscore version equal to a different version, something like one
dot 27.
So even though you have defined in your Dockerfile as one dot 26 during the build time, you can override
it with different version.
It may be one dot 27, so we can define default values for argument instructions using argument instructions
in docker file.
So these become whatever you define in Dockerfile becomes the default values and an argument variable
definition comes into effect from the line on which it is defined.
So you have defined it at this place and automatically it comes into effect from this place in Docker
file.
And another important thing to remember about argument instruction in combination with env instruction
is if you have defined env and argument both with same variable name, right?

=== Demo-20: Docker Bind Mounts: Mount in Read-Only mode ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to Dockerfile RUN and EXPOSE Instructions ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about, run and expose instructions in a Docker file.
So let's go ahead and then understand what is a run instruction in Docker file.
Run instruction will execute any commands to create a new layer on top of the current image.
So which means you take a base Docker image using from and then on top of that using run, you can install
any packages inside that Docker image.
So it creates a new layer for that.
So if we go back to our Visual Studio Code here and in zero eight Docker file run expose.
And if you go to this Docker file section right.
So here you can see you are saying you are running install curl using run right.
So we are installing the curl package inside this Alpine Slim nginx base image.
So to run that means like to install that we are using the run instruction.
So it executes any commands on top of the current image as a new layer and commits those results.
So that's the run instruction.
So the added layer is used in the next step in the Docker file.
Now one important thing to know about uh, when we are using the run instructions is cache invalidations
for run instructions.
What is the problem here?
Example like you are running something like run apk hyphen hyphen no cache, add curl.
So this is a good command.
We have already used the no cache here, but when you are running commands without that no cache means
like example.
Like when you are running these things like apt, get dist, upgrade and hyphen y.
So you are trying to upgrade your base docker image all the packages, right?
So distribution you are getting upgraded right now.
What happens is the cash, whatever is present in that image layer will not get invalidated during the
next Docker build.
So currently for the first time when you have built the docker build a docker image with v1 version.
So there it will cache all the packages there, and those will not get invalidated when you are running
the next build.
So for that, what you need to do is you can run the docker build with hyphen hyphen, no cache flag.
So when you run with hyphen, hyphen no cache.
So what happens is when you are running this command.
So whatever the cache present inside this image layer will not be taken into consideration.
So those cache will be invalidated and it will freshly run this command in our new Docker image, whatever
we are creating.
So that's one important thing to remember.
Docker build hyphen hyphen no cash.
So that's about cache invalidation for run instructions.

variable will be persisted in our container.
In simple terms, what is the key difference?
If we understand between env and arg, it is going to be env is persisted in the final image and will
be available in the container when it is run from that image.
So that's the key difference when we talk about argh!
Argh!
Is not persisted in the final image, so no scope of using that value in the container when it is run
from the image.

a single command to have it as a single image layer.

So why use arg and env together?
So this is one important thing which we need to understand.
The ARG instruction allows you to pass variables at build time without modifying the Dockerfile, making
it more dynamic and reusable.
So by combining arg and env, you can pass values configured during the build time process into the
runtime environment of the container.
So arg is used for build time customization and env is used for runtime customization.
So this separation makes the Dockerfile flexible at both stages without overlap or confusion.

=== Demo-27: Docker Compose: DEPLOY (Scale  containers) -  Nginx + UMSWeb  + MySqlDB ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to Dockerfile ENTRYPOINT instruction ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about docker file entrypoint instruction.
So this entrypoint instruction will be same as like command instruction.
And it allows you to configure a container that will run as an executable.
So it is useful for setting up a container that runs a specific command or application.
So we can use Entrypoint or command hand in hand.
But if we want to understand the clear differences.
So let's discuss them in detail.
So entrypoint versus command instructions.
So the main purpose of Entrypoint is to which it sets the main command for the container.
But when you talk about command instruction it sets the default commands or parameters.
So that's about command instruction and overriding right.
So when you want to override the entry point right can be changed with Docker run with hyphen, hyphen,
entry point, flag.
And when we talk about command instruction, if you want to override when you are running, the docker
run is by passing the arguments to docker run command.
In the previous demo itself, we have seen that how to override the command instruction.
We just passed the argument slash bin slash.
So that slash bin slash is overriding the default nginx command which we have defined in our Docker
file.
So for command instruction you can just pass the arguments.
And those will be override.
And for entry point instruction whatever you have defined in the Docker file.
So you have example like if you go to that respective demo Docker file entry point.
And here you have defined something here right.
So this entry point, if it need to be override then you need to use the hyphen hyphen entry point flag
in your docker run command.
So the common use case is like entry point will be used as a main process which rarely changes, and
for command instruction is for setting defaults or optional parameters.
And when we talk about flexibility about entrypoint and cmd in entrypoint, it is fixed and used for
primary command, and in CMD it is flexible, used as a backup command or options and example entry
point.
And you can provide program and then param and in command.
Also you can provide program example like nginx and some parameters for that nginx.
And you can also use command space without any d square brackets.
You can use param one param two something like that.
But key important thing we need to understand about entry point is it can be run as the main process
for your container.
That rarely changes.
So that is the important thing.
Now let us go ahead and then practically implement it.
So we will get a more understanding of entry point.
I will see you in the next lecture.
Until then bye bye.
Thank you.

=== Demo-29: Docker Compose: HEALTHCHECKS ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to Dockerfile HEALTHCHECK Instruction ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about health check instruction in Docker file.
So what is health check instruction in docker file.
It tells Docker how to test a container to check that it's still working.
So that's what it is going to do.
This can detect cases such as web server stuck in a loop, unable to handle new connections, even if
still it is in the running state.
So it's a very good instruction to put in to verify or verify our container health check.
Right?
So now it will have the following statuses one is starting initially during the start of the container
it will have the health check will have the starting status.
And when health check passes it will have the healthy status and it will be marked as unhealthy after
consecutive health check failures.
So any of these three statuses it is going to have.
And if you see the health check here right.
So this is the health check definition how you need to define it.
So let us see the syntax in detail.
So this health check uh you need to provide health check as the instruction.
And these are the values present inside them.
So if you go back here so here you say that interval is one argument for that or one flag timeout is
one flag start period is one flag and retry is one flag.
So interval is time between running the checks.
Default is 30s.
Uh, every 30s it will check the health check.
If you want to change it, you can change it in interval.
So time and timeout is time.
The check is allowed to run before it is considered to have failed.
So default is 30s.
You can change it and start period.
So initialization time before starting the health check.
So how much time it need to wait?
Default is zero seconds.
Example like your container takes example like 30s or one minute to start and your process should be
ready.
Some complex process, some Java application and it need to take time to start it.
So at that point of time you can change your start period to the desired amount, how much time your
application takes to start, so that after that only it will start doing the health checks.
So that is start period and retry.
Default is three.
Number of consecutive failures needed to consider the container is unhealthy.
So if continuously three times your health check fails then only it will be marked as unhealthy right.
So that's about these things okay interval timeout start period and retries.
After that you will say command and after command you will provide curl hyphen f and you will provide
your application URL.
Whatever is there.
Mine is running on port 80.
My container will be running.
Container port is 80.
So I will suggest localhost.
And then once the health check is done it is going to say exit one.
Okay.
So that is what we need to define.
If you want to have health check.
Now I have a question here in your base image itself you in the base image itself health check is present.
And that is inherited in this Docker file right.
It will inherit if it is present in the base image nginx alpine slim.
But you don't want to implement that health check in your application, but you want to use that base
image.
In that case, what you can do is you can define health check space none.
You can define so that any any health check which is inherited from the base image can be, uh, it,
uh, we don't need to if we don't want to use it, you can say none there.
So that's about health check instruction.

=== Demo-31: Docker Compose: PROFILES ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to Dockerfile USER Instruction ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about user instruction in Docker file.
So let's understand what is user instruction in a Docker file.
So this user instruction sets the default user and group ID for the rest of the stage in our build process.
So example in this Docker file we can see we have used the user instruction here which means after this
respective line whatever you see example the command instruction will use the user whatever we have
used here.
So it sets the default user and group for the rest of the stage.
So whatever the commands defined after this user instruction will use the user defined here.
And these.
This user instruction is applicable only for run Entrypoint and CMD instructions are commands.
So for run command entry command and CMD command it is going to use the user instruction.
Whatever we have set here.
One important note about this user instruction is if a user.
If a group is set only, that group applies.
Other groups are ignored and from security perspective, user context.
From security perspective.
If you look into it, running containers as non-root user enhances by minimizing the potential impact
of vulnerabilities.
So that is the very, very important thing.
So if we are running security sensitive applications, it is good to run them as non-root user.
In addition to that, by specifying a user with limited permissions, the attack service is ready.
The attack surface is reduced, making it harder for malicious actors to exploit the container.
So the best practice is to use user instruction for all the cases, wherever it is applicable.
So let us go ahead and then practically implement this and then understand it in detail.
I will see you in the next lecture.
Until then, bye bye.
Thank you.

=== Demo-33: Docker Compose: ALIASES ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to Docker Ports ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about Docker ports.
So how to use single ports and how to use multi ports.
So all those things we are going to learn.
So let's understand about port mapping with hyphen p flag.
So here p is a small case.
So this maps host ports to container ports using small p or smaller case p.
So when you use hyphen p you need to first provide your host port which is your on your local desktop
where you want to expose that container and then also provide the container port.
So this we have used in our multiple demos so far whatever we have implemented.
So it allows direct access to the container services via designated host ports.
So we will provide docker run name of your container and hyphen P.
And here 8090 is your host port which is your local desktop port.
And 80 is your container port and hyphen D.
It is going to run in detach mode.
And this is your Docker image with a tag.
That is the thing.
So important things here are.
With smaller case p we are going to map host port to container port in in our docker run command.
Now let's understand about capital case P here okay.
So use hyphen capital P to publish all exposed ports to random high numbered host ports.
So that's the important thing here.
So random high numbered host ports ideal for multi port applications where manual mapping of ports is
cumbersome.
So if you see it here here you have done a manual mapping.
This is my 8090 will be my host port mapped to the container port 80.
But if you have multiple ports in your application and those all need to be exposed on your host ports.
And if there is a means like if you are not able to manage those mappings manually, then it is easy
to use hyphen capital k, s p so that everything will be automated.
It automatically or dynamically assigns the high numbered ports, so ports are dynamically assigned
and can be viewed using Docker PS command.
So you can see docker run hyphen hyphen name and provide the demo name here a container name and this
is the capital k s p.
And we are going to run it in detached mode hyphen d.
And if you provide your Docker image automatically, it is going to assign the higher number of ports
from your local desktop for that respective container to access those services.
So let us go ahead and then practically implement this so we get a complete picture.

--- Step-01: Introduction to Docker Volumes ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about Docker storage.
In that we are going to learn about volume basics.
So let's understand these things in detail.
So volumes are the preferred mechanism for persisting data by Docker containers.
And we also have something called bind mounts.
Bind mounts are dependent on the directory structure and OS of the host machine.
Volumes are completely managed by Docker but bind mounts.
It's it's completely dependent on the OS we are using.
So let's see this picture.
So we get an idea.
So this is a in the host machine.
This is a container.
And this is our host machine file system.
And in your host machine file system whenever you create a volume it will be some space in your file
system where that volume will get created.
And this volume, this Docker area, whatever the volume related one created, is completely managed
by Docker.
That is about volumes.
But when you talk about Boyne Mounds.
Boyne Mounds are nothing but a specific path in your file system example, you have a file system slash
opt.
So in the opt for a bind mount to your container, you can take slash opt App1.
You can take as a bind mount in your container, but volume means like from your file system, some
path you can take as a storage piece using bind mounts in container, but volumes.
It can take a specific storage, but that is not from from the file system and completely managed by
Docker, which means if it is a bind mount, you can go to that path and add via your host machine or
using your container.
But when you talk about volume, volume will be completely managed inside from the container itself
means like managed by Docker only you can't manage this area separately using the host machine by going
there.
So that's the important thing to remember.
Volumes have several advantages over bind mounts.
Volumes are easier to backup or migrate than bind mounts, so that is one important one.
And you can manage volumes using Docker CLI commands or using Docker API.
In addition to that, volumes work on both Linux and Windows containers, and volumes can be safely
shared among multiple containers, and volumes will have drivers.
So these drivers let you store volumes on remote hosts or even cloud providers.
It will allow us to encrypt the contents of the volumes and also add additional functionality if needed.
So that's all these are topmost advantages, like more and more advantages of using volumes.
Then bind mounts and new volumes can have their content pre-populated by a container.
So even that is possible.
So volumes on Docker desktop have much higher performance than bind mounts from Mac and then windows
host.
Overall, it is all 99.99% cases recommended to use volumes over Bind modes, but we also have dedicated
demos for bind mounts and we are also going to implement them practically.
So if you go to the volume section here, right.
So you have a you can have bind not only volume demos, you can see volume with containers, volumes,
subdirectory, all those things you will have bind mounts and bind mounts read only and volume versus
bind mounts tmp temp file system mounts tmpfs mounts.
So all these demos we are going to implement.
So as we discussed here only volumes.

=== Demo-40: Docker Multi-stage Builds ===


--- Step-01: Introduction to populate data in volumes using containers ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to focus on two important things from Docker volumes perspective.
One is how to populate data in Docker volumes using containers, and the other is mount volumes with
read only option for even the hyphen hyphen mount flag, and also for the hyphen V flag for both the
things how to mount a volume in read only mode.
Also, we are going to learn.
So let's start with understanding about the first item here.
So what happens to data present in Docker container?
Default data from Docker image when a volume is mounted at the same path.
So this is very, very important uh thing to know from Docker volumes perspective.
Why?
Because the behavior will be very advantageous when we talk about volumes.
And when we go to bind mounts it is going to be different.
So we need to know what is the advantage of using volumes over bind mounts with this specific feature.
So let's talk about that.
You have a Docker image something like a, b, c, v one and which is a simple nginx image and it contains
some content at usr share nginx.
HTML.
Let's consider the content as all these files whatever you are seeing right.
So in this location you have it and you have built that as a Docker image.
And in the Docker image you have all this content.
Now when a container is created using this Docker image, right with new volume mounted at the same
path, what will happen.
Right.
So this is the even the volume, whatever we are creating also we are mounting at the same path.
And the behavior here is the static content from the Docker container, which is created from the Docker
image is copied to the volume automatically.
In simple terms, there is no data loss.
The volume contains the initial data from the Docker image.
So that's the advantage of using Docker volumes.
So this highlights the benefit of using volumes for data persistence in Docker, but in bind mounts.
So we have a dedicated demo for that.
But for relevance I am trying to tell in bind mounts it is not going to happen that way.
So whatever is present, whatever is initially present in the Docker image, when we create a container,
that data right will be obscured.
Means like we are not going to see that data, only the specific bind mount whatever we are going to
copy, whether it is empty or with the data, whatever it is present.
So only that data will be present or that empty bind mount will be present.
And if you try to bind mount with usr share nginx.
HTML inside that folder nothing will be present after bind mount.
So that is the disadvantage of using bind mounts if provided.
If we have existing content already present in Docker image, that will be merged with the current content
in the volume.
Even if a volume has a content in it.
So that is one thing.
And if it is a new volume, all that content, whatever is present on the image will be copied to the
new volume.
So that's the important thing and advantage of using volumes.
Now let's go ahead and then see the options about mount volume read only option.
Right.
So you have hyphen hyphen mount flag with read only access.
How we are going to do that with uh you just need to provide a read only here.
So this is the complete syntax source is equal to volume name and also target equal to container path
where you want to mount it.
And comma you just need to say read only when you are using hyphen hyphen mount flag.
And when you are using the hyphen v.
So you just need to say r o.
So colon r o.
So you can see the complete template here right.
Our syntax here volume underscore name colon container path colon row will be read only access.
So that respective volume will be mounted as read only.
So this completes the introduction about what we are going to learn in this demo.
So let's go ahead and then practically implement it.

Until then bye bye.
Thank you.

So that is about mounting a subdirectory from a volume to our container.
So now let us come back here.
So what are the advantages of using it.
It enables granular control over which parts of the volume are accessible to the container, so it is
useful for sharing specific data without exposing the entire volume content.
So we can from security perspective, it will be very helpful if we want to use what part of data to
our container, only that part of that respective volume can be mounted, not the entire volume.

--- Step-01: Introduction to Docker Bind Mounts ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about bind mounts in Docker storage.
So what are these bind mounts.
So if you see here in the picture.
So bind mounts are nothing but a file system path in your Docker host.
And then that file system path will be mounted inside your container.
So let's talk about that on a high level.
So when you use a bind mount, a file or directory on the host machine is mounted into a container.
So bind mounts have limited functionality compared to volumes.
So out of 199% cases it is recommended to use Docker volumes, then bind mounts and the file or directory
is referenced by its absolute path on the host machine.
So in the host machine at a specific file system path, if you want to mount that file system path into
your container, you can do that using bind mounts.
The file or directory does not need to exist on the Docker host already.
By contrast, when you use a volume in new directory is created within Docker storage directory on the
host machine and a docker manages the directory contents.
So completely bind mounts are different from volumes, so volumes are completely managed by Docker itself
in the Docker storage directory.
So that's about bind mounts on a high level.
Let's go ahead and then practically implement it so that we will understand it in detail.
I will see you in the next lecture.
Until then bye bye.
Thank you.

--- Step-01: Introduction to Volume vs Bind Mounts ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about volume versus bind mount in detail.
So let's go ahead and then understand the differences.
So we'll go here and talk about both Docker volumes and Docker bind mounts and differences in features
about those things how they operate.
So let's start with the data storage feature in Docker volumes and Docker bind mounts.
In Docker volumes, data is stored in Docker special directory on the host.
And if you talk about bind mounts, direct mapping of a host directory or file into the container file
system.
So we have seen both examples in detail in our previous practical demos.
And when we talk about data location stored in Docker's designated area and completely managed by Docker,
we are we will not be able to understand that or unpack it and then verify it.
But when we talk about Docker bind modes.
Any directory or file on the host system can be mounted inside our container using bind mounts, so
we can make changes from both sides.
Now data persistence.
Data persists even after the container is deleted managed by Docker.
So if you remember, when we create a volume and mount it to a container, we have seen that when container
is deleted, still volume still exists, right?
And that volume you can mount in other containers also.
So which means data persists even after the container is deleted and it is managed by Docker.
And now when we talk about the Docker bind mounts, data is tied directly to the host file.
System changes affect both the host and the container immediately.
So if we delete the container also data is present in host system only that is mounting inside the container.
There is no data loss here and we can make changes from both sides and it will be in effect if you make
the changes to your data from container, those will be reflected in the host system.
Also because it is the same path you are mounting in container.
So effect on container data.
So if you see for volumes it preserves the existing data.
So this is the very very very very important point.
And on this only this entire practical demo is going to be present.
So preserves existing data when mounted to a non-empty container directory which means in your Docker
image it's specific path.
Some content is there.
And using that you have created a container which means it's a non-empty container directory.
So existing container data is copied into the volume on first use.
So that's the advantage of using volumes.
But when you talk about bind mounts overrides container data when mounted to a non-empty container directory,
which means there is some content and for the same path inside the container, you are using, the bind
mounts to mount.
Then the host data replaces the existing data.
Your container data will be gone and whatever is present in your host system path which you are mounting,
that data will be, uh, present there.
So overwrites the container data.
And this demo, we are going to do it practically and then verify as part of this section and use cases
for Docker volumes sharing data between containers, persisting data like databases, and also for production
environments where data integrity is crucial.
And when we talk about Docker bind mounts, it is used primarily for, uh, development environments
needing live code changes.
So in your host file system where you are making your changes there in the container, those changes
will reflect immediately.
So for testing configurations accessing host files from the container.
So for those scenarios you can use bind mounts and performance.
If you see generally optimized by Docker for better performance and reliability.
For Docker volumes, it is going to be highly performant.
And when we talk about bind mounts, it depends on the host file system and it may vary.
We cannot guarantee the performance of it.
And when we talk about security Docker volumes are more secure when compared to bind mounts.
Bind mounts are less secure because Docker volumes are isolated from the host file system, and those
are managed by Docker.
But when we talk about Docker bind mounts, they are less secure.
Grants the container direct access to the host file system.
So that's the difference between volume versus bind mounts.
And if you go back here this is the thing effect on container data.
Docker volumes preserves existing data and Docker bind mounts uh overrides container data.
Based on these points.
We are going to do a practical demo now.
So I will see you in the next lecture.
Until then bye bye.
Thank you.

--- Step-01: Introduction to Docker tmpfs mount ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about Tmpfs mount or a temporary mount in Docker storage.
So when we compare this Tmpfs mounts right.
These temporary mounts with the uh Docker uh regular volume and bind mounts.
We need to understand few things clearly here.
Volume and bind mounts let you share files between the host machine and container so that you can persist
data even after the container is stopped.
So that is possible in bind mounts and volumes.
But when we talk about tmpfs mounts right.
So these mounts are temporary file storage systems so that reside in host system's memory.
So because of that when the container is stopped right.
So it is going to lose all the thing whatever you have stored in that uh tmpfs mount.
So when you start the container back, the data is not present.
So the data is always stored in the host memory.
So this tmpfs mount is nothing but a part of your Ram from your host is mounted there to store the files,
so they are useful for scenarios requiring fast ephemeral storage that doesn't persist after the container
stops at Tmpfs Mount is temporary and only persisted in the host memory.
When the container stops, the tmpfs mount is removed and files written there won't be persisted.
So that's the thing.
So the key difference here is when we compare that with volume and bind mounts in volume and bind mounts,
data is persisted in the host system.
Even the container is stopped and even it is deleted.
But in tmpfs mount, because the data is stored in the host memory, when the container is stopped,
the data is lost.
When you start the container back, the data is not present.
So we are going to test this explicitly and understand it in more detail in a practical way.
I will see you in the next lecture.
Until then, bye bye.
Thank you.

And additionally there are some tmpfs options.
One is how much of the Ram you can allocate for your tmpfs mount in your container.
So that is also one very good option.
By default, Tmpfs mounts have a maximum size of 50% of the host's total Ram.
You can customize this using the Tmpfs size option.
So when you are creating the command docker run hyphen hyphen name Tmpfs demo size.
And in the mount you say type is Tmpfs destination is slash app, and for slash app, you want to allocate

So now from your whole 3.8 GB of your Ram in your host machine, only 100 MB is allocated, 100 MB is
allocated.
So this is a safest option.
Why?
Because when we are allocating if without sys, if you try to mount the host system's memory to your
container, and if there is a huge amount of data is dumped inside that temporary folder.
Right.
So it might, uh, it might create a problem with your host system's memory is completely consumed and
your entire system might crash, or it might slow down, or everything will be a mess.
So using this tmpfs sys option, we can allocate whatever is needed.
So that is the reason this is one very good important option for us.
So that is about it.
So we also have a lot of limitations of tmpfs mounts.
So those are nothing but ephemeral.
Storage is one important thing.
So data stored in tmpfs mounts does not persist after the container stops, so it is not suitable for
data that needs to be retained across container restarts and there is no sharing between containers.
Unlike Docker volumes, tmpfs mounts cannot be shared between multiple containers, and also memory
consumption is another bigger limitation here.
These tmpfs mounts consume Ram.
Excessive use can lead to memory exhaustion on the host.
So these are the three important limitations with Tmpfs mounts.

--- Step-01: Introduction to deploying multi-container apps using Docker commands ---

Welcome back.

So if you see here we have a simple MySQL database.
And here we have a simple some web application.
We call it as user management web application.
So we deploy user management web application in one container and MySQL database in one container.
And so now these are two containers.
So like this.
There is another feature in your application some Redis cache.
And you want to deploy one more container.
So like this we can deploy multiple containers.
And then how to connect those containers together to communicate.

So all those things we are going to see in this demo using Docker commands.
And once we learn to do that using Docker commands, in the next demo onwards, we will focus on doing
that declaratively using YAML using Docker compose a tool.
So now let's start with learning to do that using docker run command okay.
So now if you see in general one container can run one process at a time.
So example a container can run nginx process or a container can run MySQL database process.
Or if it is some spring boot or some Java application.
So that container can run that specific process in that container.
Now use cases here are we are going to deploy a simple application that will create users in MySQL database.
So this is a simple application.
You can log into that application and create users.
And those users will get created in this MySQL database and we need two containers for that.
One is to run the web application which is user management web application, and the other is database
which is MySQL database.
So separate containers, if we put them in separate containers, they let us version them separately
and also update those versions in isolation.
So if you have a application code changes and if you want to deploy a V2 version of your code, then
you need to update it only this respective container means like from the Docker image v2 version tag,
you can create a new container and this can be terminated.
So no impact to the database.
So that way separate containers let you version and update versions in isolation.
And by default containers run in isolation and don't know anything about other processes or containers
on the same mission.
So if you are running user management, web application.
So this user management web application need to communicate to this MySQL database to create the users
inside it, or to create whatever the objects this, uh, application does.
Those things need to be created in this database.
So for that purpose, we need to put them in a same network.
So how do you allow one container to talk to another.
So the answer there is container networking.
So if you place the two containers on the same network they can talk to each other.
So if it is container one and container two, if these two are present in the same network then they
can talk to each other.

--- Step-02: Create Docker network and mysqldb container ---

--- Step-01: Introduction to Docker compose basics ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn the basics of Docker Compose and also implement a simple Docker compose
YAML configuration for creating a MySQL database.
So let's understand what exactly is this docker compose?
Before going and understanding what is the use case we are going to implement.
So Docker compose is a simple tool used for defining and running multi-container applications.
So it helps for an efficient development and deployment experience.
Compose simplifies the control of your entire application stack, making it easy to manage services,
networks and volumes in a single YAML configuration file.
So let us go back here to our Visual Studio Code and understand that in a detailed way.
So we have multiple Docker compose demos here.
If you go to one of the around something like demo 30 right in the startup order demo.
So if you see here we have multiple things defined here in services.
You have a ingenix defined here.
And you have the app UMS defined here, and also a database defined here.
So you have defined three things here.
One is a web server and the other is a app server, and the other is a database server.
And with a single command, all these things can be ready for us by just running a single command.
Whereas in the previous demo.
So if you go to the previous demo here.
Right.
So let me go back to demo 23.
Right.
So if we go to demo 23, when creating a Multi-container application, first we have created a network.

And then after that we created the user management web app container.
In addition to that, if you want to front this application with nginx, then you need to create one
more container by running this command.
So which means you need to run multiple commands to create all these things.
But with Docker compose, all these things will be completely simplified.
Just a docker.
Write this YAML configuration file for each service.
What is needed?
And after that, if you run the docker compose up as the command, then automatically all these things
will get created immediately.

integration workflows.

example like starting, stopping and rebuilding the services.

So whenever the service is created, when whenever you have run the docker compose up, it has created
a network called UMS stack default one network and all the three services, whatever you have seen all
the three services app, UMS, DB, MySQL and web nginx all is present in the single network, which
means all are able to communicate to each other, right?
Which means from web nginx.
Also you will be able to communicate to database.
So it is a security threat right.
So you are opening your connections from web to DB very openly in a single network.
But instead of that using Docker networks, you can effectively design wherein from your web tier you
will not be able to access your DB tier only via app tier.
Only you can access your DB tier.
So such type of designs you can do using Docker Network.
So let us see what we are going to do here.
So we will define two networks here.
One is front end network and second is back end network.
And if you see here db MySQL we will point it to the back end network.
And after that we will go to app tier.
And in app tier we will in the network section we will say both front end and back end.
So from this app comes it will be able to communicate to DB MySQL because it is in the back end network.
In the same way it also has the front end network.
And here also you can see in web nginx you have the front end.
Network added.
So from front end network from nginx nginx can communicate to.
This app comes because this app comes is present in front end network also.
So.
App tier is connected to front end network and app tier is connected to.
Also the back end network.
So which means from app comes you can send the.
Traffic to DB MySQL to access your database.
And from web nginx you can send the request to app comes to access your application.
So this can communicate to both.
Tiers.
But this web cannot access DB tier directly.
Your database is 100% secure.
So this web tier even doesn't know what is DB tier network at all.
You need to only it can only access the app tier related applications.
So that's what isolation of uh isolation isolating our applications using Docker networks.

--- Step-01: Introduction to Docker Compose startup order with conditions ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to implement startup order with conditional use case in Docker compose.
So what exactly that means right.
So if you see here app comes and here you have set depends on DB MySQL.
But in addition to that you are also going to define a condition here.
So earlier when you said depends on and db MySQL first db MySQL will be started.
Only after that db MySQL is started, then app comes will be started.
It is only for starting the services.
Okay, but important thing here is you want to start the app related containers only after dbms db MySQL
is healthy.
So for that purpose you can define a condition stating service underscore healthy.
So in this case what happens is once the db MySQL is started and once that container is in the healthy
status, after that only this app ums start will trigger and once the app UMS is started, right?
And it will connect to the DB and also come online.
So means like it's a sequential order where how you want to start your applications.
In the same way, if you go to the web tier here for a web service right here, you said depends on
app comes.
So app comes is the service and condition service healthy.
So after app UMC started and it is marked as healthy using this health check right then only this web
nginx will start.
So first DB will start.
And once it is healthy then only app ums will start.
And once app UMS is healthy then only web nginx will be started.
So like this.
The startup order of your services in your Docker compose you can control using depends on with conditions
right?
It depends on.
You can define conditions and with service healthy option you can control the entire startup order of
your application.
So I'll see you in the next lecture with a practical demo for this.
Until then, bye bye.
Thank you.

--- Step-01: Introduction to Docker compose PROFILES ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about Docker compose profiles.
So let's understand what exactly profiles mean.
So these Docker compose profiles help us in customizing services for our for different environments.
So for example like we can enable specific services in our Docker compose file at specific need.
So selective service activation is the key feature for these profiles.
So we use these profiles to enable or disable specific service based on your environment or use case.
So if we understand that with a practical example it will be easy.
So if you see it here this is a service named NetSuite.
So I have given some name here.
You can give any name.
So here in this Docker image you will have lot of troubleshooting tools.
And you have we have given the name of this container as UMS NetSuite and we are saying this container
should run at infinity.
Once it is started, you can connect to that container and once you connect to the terminal of that
container, right?
What happens is there are multiple Linux tools available for you for troubleshooting.
So what we have done is we set the service name as NetSuite.
We have provided the image container name and we are saying that it need to run to infinity.
It should not be means like until and unless it is terminated it should be running this respective container.
And here is the important thing.
We said that it is profiles and it comes under debug profile.
So anything you can give name here any desired name you can give I have given my desired name as debug
and this NetSuite service will be accessible.
Means like attached to both front end and back end networks.
So you can connect to this NetSuite container.
And then from there you can troubleshoot any issues with your containers or services present in your
front end network and also in the back end network.
So now if you see it here, assign services to profiles.
Unassigned services run by default.
So what it is trying to say here is when you use the profiles argument, what happens is whatever you
have defined the service in your Docker compose file with this profiles thing, right?
That will not be started by default.
When you run docker compose up hyphen d, you need to explicitly go there and then start it like this
docker compose hyphen hyphen profile and provide your profile name and up, and then hyphen d.
Then only this service in the docker compose file will start.
So if you go back to our Visual Studio Code here and in demo 31, in profiles demo you have the Docker
compose YAML file.
So you have the web nginx, you have the app ums and you have the db MySQL.
And in addition to that you have something called a new service which is called NetSuite.
Right.
So this it is associated with profiles.
So when you say docker compose up right and only web and then app and then db will be started.
And this.
Net should service whatever the container present under this net service will not should not start okay.
Why.
It is not going to not start because it needs explicitly starting it all the default services will start,
but only services which are assigned with the profiles argument.
So those will be started by providing hyphen hyphen profile and provide the profile name.
So when you say docker compose hyphen, hyphen profile and debug and up, then what happens is this
respective service related containers will be started.
So profile assigned services start only when their profile is activated.
So if you want to stop them also you can say docker compose hyphen hyphen profile debug is the profile
name and then down and important thing here is it includes optional services for means like it is,
uh, primarily used for having the more flexibility in our Docker compose file.
So you can add these type of services which are required for troubleshooting R, which are like some,
uh, some tools where like you want to install for build process and then only be used when you want
in need, then you can define them, but you can provide the respective profile for them.
Means like add the profiles argument and give the name so that they will not get started as default
services.
They need to be explicitly started when there is a need, so activate these services as needed without
modifying the compose file.
So that's the important thing here.
So that's about very high level on a Docker compose profiles.

--- Step-01: Introduction to Docker Compose LINKS ---

In this demo we are going to learn about Docker compose links.
So let's understand what exactly are these links in default networking behavior in Docker.
Right.
So when we run services using Docker compose it automatically creates a default network for our application.
And all services are connected to this network and can reach each other using their service names.
So this we have seen in our previous demos.
Also like if you see app ums can be accessed from web, nginx and web nginx can be accessed from app
UMS before defining specific networks.
Right.
So now because it creates a default network and in addition to that, communication without links is
like using the app UMS and then web, uh, web, nginx, whatever the names we have, right without
links you the how it is going to work is you access your service names.
Right.
So example like you have the DB MySQL service name and that you have given in the DB hostname field
here using service names we access it right.
So without links.
Now with links what happens?
We are going to see purpose of links.
The links option is used only to create additional aliases or host names for a service.
So example you have a DB MySQL service here right?
So let us go to the links section here.
And in links demo open the docker compose YAML file.
And if you come down here this is the DB MySQL service.
And this using this name you can call it in your app UMS.
But if you want to call it with a different name, right.

service with a different name there you can define the links section here.
So here in app UMS you can define a link section here links argument.
And then for DB MySQL service you can call it as my ums db or for that same db MySQL service you can
call it as some.
My db db.
So whatever the desired names you want for this db MySQL service, you can give it here.

Also here instead of db MySQL you can give my ums, db my db db, whatever you want you can give and
inside the application also when you are writing the code and there if you are given a different name
there and then it is hard coded somewhere or whatever it is using the links, you can give those names
here, right?
So which means the advantage of using it is like you can give alternate names for your service using
this links option.
And very important thing to remember is it does not enable communication.
It only provides alternative names by which a service can be reached from another service.
That is all.
And in addition to that, in the following example you can see for the db MySQL we have given two alternate
names, my ums db and my db db.
And one very important thing which we need to remember about these links is links is a old technology

--- Step-01: Introduction to Docker Compose ALIASES ---

Welcome back.
In this demo we are going to learn about Docker compose aliases.
So these aliases declare alternative host names for the service on the network.
So these are network scoped.
So other containers on the same network can use either the service name or an alias to connect to one
of the service containers.
So if you see here right so we have the networks defined in our app UM's front end and back end networks
means like this app UMS is associated with front end network and back end network.
And inside them means like inside the front end.
You have given the aliases for this app UMS service as UMS app and dev UMS app and for back end right
means like for the containers which is in the app which is associated with back end network.
They can access it as my spring app or my API services.
So aliases are network scoped.
The same service can have different aliases on different networks.
So in the back end network, the containers which are attached to the back end network, they can access
the same app EMI service with these two names and the containers which are attached to the front end
network they can access with UMS app and dev UMS app.
So how we are going to test it?
So if you we will connect to web nginx which is a front end, uh, front end network associated service
web nginx.
And from there, if you try to access these two things, it should resolve.
But these two things should not be resolved in the same way.
If you go to the MySQL DB MySQL service.
So for that service only the back end is associated from there.
If you access this app UMS service with these two names, it should be accessible my spring app and
my API services.
But when you try to access with UMS app or dev UMS app, it should not be accessible.
So these things we are going to test it practically now.
I will see you in the next lecture.
Until then, bye bye.
Thank you.

--- Step-02: Implement Docker Compose BUILD demo ---

But one more time.
If you want to reiterate, we can use this build flag for any code changes or Docker file changes or
environment updates.
And in addition to that, this build flag helps us very importantly in tackling the automatic versus
manual building things.
So what happens is when you run docker compose up, it will use the existing image present in your in
your desktop, or if it need to be downloaded from Docker Hub, it will download and then it will start
the container, but it won't check for the changes in your docker file, what you have newly updated,
or any code changes you have done in your code.
Respective repositories or in code respective folders.
And it is not going to check anything there.
so it is going to only use the existing image.
But when you use hyphen, hyphen build flag, what happens is it will rebuild the image regardless of
whether it was previously built or not.
So when it rebuilds the new Docker image using the Docker image only, it is going to create a container,
which means you always have the latest code available for you.
So that helps a lot when you use this hyphen.
